Title: Doubly Robust Proxy Causal Learning with Neural Mean Embeddings

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.09514v1

Published Time: Tue, 12 May 2026 01:34:23 GMT

Number of Pages: 87

Markdown Content:
# Doubly Robust Proxy Causal Learning with Neural Mean Embeddings 

Bariscan Bozkurt, Alexandre Galashov, Dimitri Meunier, Zikai Shen, Arthur Gretton, Houssam Zenati University College London May 12, 2026 

Abstract 

Unobserved confounding prevents standard covariate adjustment from identifying causal response func-tions in observational studies. Proxy causal learning addresses this problem through bridge equations involving treatment- and outcome-inducing proxies, avoiding direct recovery of the latent confounder. Existing doubly robust proxy estimators combine outcome and treatment bridges, but typically rely on fixed kernels, sieves, or low-dimensional semiparametric models; existing neural proxy methods are more flexible, but are largely single-bridge estimators. We develop a neural doubly robust framework for proxy causal learning with continuous and structured treatments. Our method introduces a neural mean-embedding estimator for the treatment bridge, combines it with a neural outcome bridge, and estimates the doubly robust correction through a final regression stage. The framework covers population, heterogeneous, and conditional dose-response functions, yielding full response-curve estimators rather than binary-treatment effects. The algorithms use two stages for each bridge and history-aware updates of the final linear layers to stabilize stochastic multi-stage training. We prove consistency of the algorithms showing that the doubly robust error is controlled by the final averaging and regression errors together with the smaller of the outcome- and treatment-side weak-norm bridge errors. Across synthetic and image-valued benchmarks, the proposed estimators outperform existing baselines and single-bridge neural estimators, showing the benefit of combining learned outcome and treatment bridges in a doubly robust construction. Our implementation is available at https://github.com/BariscanBozkurt/DRPCL-Neural-Mean-Embedding .

# 1 Introduction and related works 

Causal inference from observational data is difficult when treatment assignment is confounded. Standard adjustment assumes that all relevant confounders are observed, yielding Y (a) ⊥ A | X under consistency, no interference, and positivity. This condition underlies covariate adjustment and propensity-score methods [ 1, 2, 3], outcome-regression methods [ 4], and representation-learning estimators for treatment effects [ 5, 6]. In practice, however, important determinants of treatment and outcome may be unrecorded, noisy, or only indirectly measured. Instrumental-variable methods offer one route beyond observed-confounder adjustment [7, 8, 9], but require exclusion and independence assumptions that are often restrictive. Proxy causal learning (PCL) uses a different source of information: proxy variables that are informative about the unobserved confounding [ 10 , 11 , 12 ]. In the standard PCL setting, observed variables are partitioned into covariates X, treatment proxies Z, and outcome proxies W . As illustrated in Figure 1, the treatment A and outcome Y are confounded by observed covariates X and latent variables U , while Z and W provide treatment-side and outcome-side information about the same latent confounding. Under conditional independence and completeness assumptions, causal effects can be identified without recovering U directly. Identification is instead expressed through bridge functions: functions of the observed proxies, treatment, and covariates whose conditional expectations reproduce the relevant regression or weighting equations. Learning these bridges replaces latent-confounder inference by the solution of Fredholm integral equations of the first kind. 1

> arXiv:2605.09514v1 [cs.LG] 10 May 2026

Existing PCL estimators mainly follow outcome-bridge or treatment-bridge routes. Outcome-bridge methods learn a function h(A, X, W ) satisfying E{Y − h(A, X, W ) | A, X, Z } = 0 , and then recover response curves by averaging or conditioning the learned bridge. Linear and semiparametric bridge estimators were developed in proximal causal inference [ 12 , 13 ]; sieve and proxy-control estimators were studied by Deaner [14] ; and RKHS-based methods provide flexible nonparametric bridge estimators [ 15 , 16 ]. Neural outcome-bridge estimators replace fixed features with learned representations, improving scalability to nonlinear and high-dimensional settings [ 17 , 18 ]. These methods estimate the regression-type bridge h(A, X, W ), but leave open the neural estimation of the complementary weighting-type bridge based on Z.   

> U
> ZXW
> AY

Figure 1: Causal graph for PCL setting. Treatment bridges provide the complementary weighting route. For a given causal estimand, a treatment bridge φ(A, X, Z ) is characterized by a moment equation of the form E{r0(A, X, W ) −

φ(A, X, Z ) | A, X, W } = 0 , where r0 denotes the estimand specific density ratio; for the population dose-response curve, r0(a, x, w ) = 

pA(a)/p A|X,W (a | x, w ). This makes treatment bridges analogous to inverse-weighting functions [ 2 , 19 ]. Semiparametric [ 13 ], minimax [ 20 ], and kernel-based treatment-bridge estimators [ 21 ] have been developed for proxy causal inference, including extensions to contin-uous treatments. Recent neural treatment-bridge work moves toward adaptive treatment-side estimation [ 22 ], but is developed for binary treatment effects rather than full response functions with continuous or structured treatments. Doubly robust PCL combines the outcome- and treatment-bridge routes, mirroring classical doubly robust causal inference, where validity can be retained when one of two complementary components is correctly specified [ 23 , 24 , 25 ]. This is especially useful in PCL because the two bridges use the proxies differently, and it is rarely known in advance whether W or Z is more informative about the latent confounding. Semiparametric and kernel doubly robust proxy estimators have been developed [ 13 , 26 , 27 ], but they are either formulated for discrete-treatment contrasts or rely on fixed kernel representations for continuous treatments. This leaves open a neural doubly robust PCL framework that learns both outcome- and treatment-side representations for continuous and structured response functions. We develop a neural doubly robust PCL framework for estimating causal response functions, including population, heterogeneous, and conditional dose-response targets. On the outcome side, we build on two-stage neural bridge regression. On the treatment side, we introduce a neural mean-embedding estimator that learns conditional embeddings of the treatment proxy using adaptive features. We then combine the two learned bridges through a final regression stage that estimates the doubly robust correction. The resulting framework covers population dose-response, heterogeneous dose-response, and conditional dose-response targets, and is designed for continuous and structured treatments. To stabilize stochastic multi-stage training, we use history-aware updates of the final linear heads, inspired by Galashov et al. [28]. Our contributions are: 

• We extend doubly robust bridge identification beyond population dose-response curves to heterogeneous and conditional response functions. 

• We introduce a neural mean-embedding treatment bridge and combine it with a neural outcome bridge to obtain a doubly robust PCL estimator for continuous and structured treatments. 

• We propose DRPCLNET-V1 and DRPCLNET-V2, two stable multi-stage training algorithms with history-aware closed-form linear-head updates and show significant practical gains over single-bridge neural estimators and existing proxy baselines on synthetic and image-valued benchmarks. 

• We prove the consistency of our proposed estimators showing that the doubly robust error decomposes into final averaging/regression errors and the smaller of the outcome- and treatment-side weak-norm bridge errors. The remainder of the paper is organized as follows. Section 2 introduces the causal targets. Section 3 gives outcome-bridge, treatment-bridge, and doubly robust identification results. Section 4 presents the neural mean 2embedding estimators and the resulting doubly robust algorithms. Section 6 reports the numerical experiments, and Section 7 concludes. 

# 2 Problem setup and target causal functions 

This section introduces the causal targets studied in the paper within the proxy causal learning framework shown in Figure 1. 

Notation. Uppercase letters denote random variables and calligraphic letters denote their state spaces; lowercase letters denote realizations. We observe O = ( Y, A, X, Z, W ) ∼ P, where A ∈ A is the treatment, 

Y ∈ Y is the outcome, X ∈ X denotes observed covariates, Z ∈ Z is a treatment proxy, and W ∈ W is an outcome proxy. We write PR and PR|T for marginal probability laws and conditional laws, and E[·] for expectation. When densities or probability mass functions exist, we write them with lowercase p, for example 

pA(a) and pA|X,W (a | x, w ).Let A ∈ A denote the treatment, which may be continuous and high dimensional, and let Y ∈ Y denote the observed outcome. Let X ∈ X denote the vector of observed covariates, and let U ∈ U denote an unobserved confounder. Write X = ( S, V ), where V ∈ V is the pre-specified part of the observed covariates used to define groups, and S ∈ S contains the remaining observed covariates. For example, in a clinical study, V could include age, sex, or baseline severity. A value V = v defines the group whose response curve we want to estimate. Our goal is to identify and estimate causal functions describing how the outcome changes under interventions on A,both at the population level and within such V = v groups. When this decomposition is not needed, we write 

X for the full observed covariate vector. We now define the causal targets studied in the paper. 

Definition 2.1 (Target causal parameters) . Let Y (a) denote the potential outcome under the intervention 

A = a. We consider: (i) Dose-response curve: fATE (a) := E[Y (a)].(ii) Heterogeneous dose-response: fCATE (a, v ) := E[Y (a) | V = v].(iii) Conditional dose-response: fATT (a, a ′) := E[Y (a) | A = a′].

The subscripts follow standard discrete-treatment terminology: ATE, CATE, and ATT refer to average, conditional average, and average-on-the-treated treatment effects. Here, they index the corresponding re-sponse functions rather than discrete-treatment contrasts. Under consistency, Y (a) = Y whenever A = a,and latent exchangeability, Y (a) ⊥ A | X, U , these targets admit the latent-variable representations: (i) 

fATE (a) = E[E[Y | A = a, X, U ]] , (ii) fCATE (a, v ) = E[E[Y | A = a, S, V = v, U ] | V = v], and (iii) 

fATT (a, a ′) = E[E[Y | A = a, X, U ] | A = a′]. These representations show that the causal functions would be identified by adjustment if the latent confounder U were observed. Proxy causal learning instead identifies the same functions without observing or estimating U , by using the proxies Z and W to learn bridge functions. Intuitively, Z is informative about the latent confounding through treatment assignment, while W is informative about the same latent confounding through the outcome. We formalize these proxy roles through the following conditional independence assumptions, and then impose completeness to ensure that the proxies carry enough information for bridge identification. 

Assumption 2.2 (Proxy conditional independencies) . Assume that the data are generated by a structural causal model compatible with Figure 1, and that the following conditional independencies hold: (i) Y ⊥ Z | U, A, X ,(ii) W ⊥ Z | U, A, X, and W ⊥ A | U, X. 

Assumption 2.2 formalizes the roles of Z and W as treatment and outcome proxies, respectively. In addition, identification requires that these proxies be sufficiently informative about the latent confounder U conditional on (A, X ) [11, 16]. This is captured by the following completeness assumption. 

Assumption 2.3 (Completeness) . For PA,X -almost all (a, x ) ∈ A × X , and for every square-integrable function 

ℓ ∈ L2(PU |A=a,X =x): (i) E[ℓ(U ) | A = a, X = x, Z ] = 0 PZ|A=a,X =x-a.e. ⇐⇒ ℓ(U ) = 0 PU |A=a,X =x-a.e. , (ii) 

E[ℓ(U ) | A = a, X = x, W ] = 0 PW |A=a,X =x-a.e. ⇐⇒ ℓ(U ) = 0 PU |A=a,X =x-a.e. .

3In the next section, we show how these assumptions lead to outcome-bridge, treatment-bridge, and doubly robust identification formulas for the target causal functions. 

# 3 Identification of causal functions 

This section gives identification formulas for the causal functions in Definition 2.1. We first review the outcome-bridge route, then introduce treatment bridges as inverse-weighting analogues, and finally combine both routes to obtain doubly robust formulas. 

3.1 Outcome bridge identification 

Outcome-bridge identification is the standard route in PCL [ 11 , 15 , 17 , 18 ]: it seeks a function h(A, X, W )

such that E[h(A, X, W ) | A, X, Z ] = E[Y | A, X, Z ]. Once such a bridge exists, response functions are obtained by averaging or conditioning h over the relevant observed distribution. 

Theorem 3.1 (Identification via the outcome bridge) . Suppose Assumptions 2.2 and 2.3 hold. Fur-thermore, suppose that for PA,X -almost all (a, x ) ∈ A × X , there exists an outcome bridge function 

h0(a, x, ·) ∈ L2 PW |A=a,X =x

 such that 

E[Y | A = a, X = x, Z ] = E[h0(a, x, W ) | A = a, X = x, Z ], PZ|A=a,X =x-a.e. (1) 

Then, the causal functions are identified by (i) fATE (a) = E[h0(a, X, W )] , (ii) fCATE (a, v ) = E[h0(a, X, W ) |

V = v], (iii) fATT (a, a ′) = E[h0(a, X, W ) | A = a′].

The dose-response formula is the standard proxy outcome-bridge result of Miao et al. [11] . The heterogeneous and conditional formulas follow by conditioning the same bridge representation on V = v or A = a′, as in Singh [16, Theorem 1]. 

3.2 Treatment bridge identification 

We next turn to treatment bridge identification, which complements the outcome-bridge view and is closer in spirit to inverse propensity weighting [ 2, 19 ]. This perspective has been developed in semiparametric, minimax, and kernel forms, including recent extensions to continuous treatments and density-ratio-free formulations [13, 20, 26, 21, 27]. It is also the identification route that will motivate our treatment-side estimator. 

Theorem 3.2 (Identification via treatment bridge) . Suppose Assumptions 2.2 and 2.3 hold. Assume that for PA,X -almost all (a, x ) ∈ A × X , there exist treatment bridge functions φATE 0 (a, x, ·) ∈ L2 PZ|A=a,X =x



and, for any a′ ∈ A , φATT 0 (a, a ′, x, ·) ∈ L2 PZ|A=a,X =x

. In addition, assume that for PA,S,V -almost all 

(a, s, v ) ∈ A × S × V , there exists φCATE 0 (a, v, s, ·) ∈ L2 PZ|A=a,S =s,V =v

. Suppose these bridge functions satisfy, almost everywhere with respect to the corresponding conditional law of W ,

E[φATE 0 (a, X, Z ) | A = a, X, W ] = pA(a)/p A|X,W (a | X, W ), (2) 

E[φCATE 0 (a, v, S, Z ) | A = a, S, V = v, W ] = pA|V (a | V = v)/p A|S,V,W (a | S, V = v, W ),

E[φATT 0 (a, a ′, X, Z ) | A = a, X, W ] = pX,W |A(X, W | a′)/p X,W |A(X, W | a).

Then, the causal functions are identified by (i) fATE (a) = E[Y φ ATE 0 (a, X, Z ) | A = a], (ii) fCATE (a, v ) = 

E[Y φ CATE 0 (a, v, S, Z ) | A = a, V = v], (iii) fATT (a, a ′) = E[Y φ ATT 0 (a, a ′, X, Z ) | A = a].

The ATE and ATT statements follow from the treatment-bridge identification results of Bozkurt et al. [21] .We prove the CATE extension in Appendix A. 

3.3 Doubly robust identification 

Combining outcome and treatment bridges yields doubly robust formulas that remain valid when either bridge is correctly specified and underlie semiparametric and kernel DR proxy methods [ 13 , 26 , 27 ]. The population 4dose-response formula recovers existing results; the heterogeneous and conditional formulas are new extensions developed here. The doubly robust identification theorem below extends the bridge structure that appears in the efficient influence functions derived in Appendix C for discrete treatments. That appendix treats A as discrete, and 

V as discrete for the heterogeneous target. Here, we state the corresponding bridge identities directly as identification formulas for general treatments and covariates, including continuous A and V .

Theorem 3.3 (Doubly robust identification) . Suppose Assumptions 2.2 and 2.3 hold, and suppose that the outcome bridge h0 from Theorem 3.1 and the treatment bridge functions from Theorem 3.2 exist. Then the target causal functions admit the doubly robust representations 

f (DR) ATE (a; h0, φ ATE 0 ) = E[φATE 0 (a, X, Z ){Y − h0(a, X, W )} | A = a] + E[h0(a, X, W )] , (3) 

f (DR) CATE (a, v ; h0, φ CATE 0 ) = E[φCATE 0 (a, v, S, Z ){Y − h0(a, X, W )} | A = a, V = v]+ E[h0(a, X, W ) | V = v],f (DR) ATT (a, a ′; h0, φ ATT 0 ) = E[φATT 0 (a, a ′, X, Z ){Y − h0(a, X, W )} | A = a]+ E[h0(a, X, W ) | A = a′].

Moreover, each formula identifies the corresponding causal function if either h0 is a valid outcome bridge or the corresponding treatment bridge function is valid. 

The ATE representation in Eq. 3 is the covariate-adjusted analogue of the doubly robust dose-response formula in Bozkurt et al. [27] . The CATE and ATT representations are new extensions; their derivations are given in Appendix B. 

# 4 Neural mean embedding approach for dose-response estimation 

We introduce neural estimators for dose-response estimation that retain the staged bridge-learning structure of proxy identification while replacing the fixed kernel and sieve feature maps used in existing estimators [ 15 , 16 , 21 ] with adaptive trainable representations; fixed-feature baselines are reviewed in Appendix D. The framework consists of an outcome-bridge network, a treatment-bridge network, and a final doubly robust regression stage. We give the core parameterizations and objectives here, and defer stochastic training details and update rules to Appendices E.1, E.2, and E.3. 

Setup and notation. Both bridge estimators use a two-stage regression structure followed by a final third stage. We use superscript (h) for the outcome bridge and (φ) for the treatment bridge. Given a dataset 

D = {(ai, x i, z i, w i, y i)}ni=1 , we form two disjoint folds for each bridge. For the outcome bridge, we split it into D(h)1 = {( ¯ai, ¯xi, ¯zi, ¯wi)}nh

Training schedule. Both bridge networks are trained by alternating between updating the featurizers by gradient descent and updating the linear heads by their closed-form history-centered ridge solutions. Importantly, the proxy-side second-stage featurizer, ϕ(h) 

> W, 2

for OutcomeNet or ϕ(φ) 

> Z, 2

for TreatmentNet, affects the first-stage regression target. Therefore, during the second-stage feature update, gradients are propagated through the closed-form first-stage solution for V , following the deep feature learning strategy of Xu et al. [17 , 30] . For squared losses, the second-stage linear head has a closed-form history-centered ridge update. For differentiable robust losses such as Huber or log-cosh [ 31 ], the closed-form ridge update for the linear head is replaced by a small number of L-BFGS steps [ 32 ]. This training schedule preserves the two-stage bridge structure while allowing flexible neural representations and robust second-stage objectives. 6Doubly robust unification. We combine OutcomeNet and TreatmentNet via Eq. 3 via two novel neural implementations, unlike Bozkurt et al. [27] who use fixed kernel features. Our first implementation, that we call Doubly Robust PCL Network-V1 (DRPCLNET-V1), directly learns the bridge-weighted residual correction. Given fitted bridges ˆh and ˆφ, we construct pseudo-outcomes 

y(κ, 1)  

> i

= ˆ φ(ai, x i, z i){yi − ˆh(ai, x i, w i)}, and fit a regression network k(κ, 1) (·; θ(κ)1 ) such that 

k(κ, 1) (a; θ(κ)1 ) ≈ E[ ˆ φ(a, X, Z ){Y − ˆh(a, X, W )} | A = a]. (5) The resulting estimator is ˆf (DR1) ATE (a) = ˆf (h)ATE (a) + k(κ, 1) (a; θ(κ)1 ).

Our second implementation, DRPCLNET-V2 , uses the decomposition 

f (DR) ATE (a) = E[h0(a, X, W )] + E[Y φ 0(a, X, Z ) | A = a] − E[φ0(a, X, Z )h0(a, X, W ) | A = a].

OutcomeNet estimates the first term and TreatmentNet estimates the second. We learn only the inter-action term by constructing pseudo-outcomes y(κ, 2)  

> i

= ˆφ(ai, x i, z i) ˆh(ai, x i, w i), and fitting k(κ, 2) (·; θ(κ)2 ) to approximate k(κ, 2) (a; θ(κ)2 ) ≈ E[ ˆφ(a, X, Z ) ˆh(a, X, W ) | A = a]. The resulting estimator is ˆf (DR2) ATE (a) = ˆf (h)ATE (a) + f (φ)(a; θ(φ)3 ) − k(κ, 2) (a; θ(κ)2 ). Full algorithmic details for both variants are given in Appendix E.3. The CATE and ATT estimators follow the same multi-stage construction with target-specific treatment bridges and conditional response regressions. Details are deferred to Appendices F and G. 

# 5 Consistency results 

We present the consistency result for population dose-response estimation, focusing on DRPCLNET-V1. Similarly, DRPCLNET-V2 follows from the same decomposition after replacing the residual correction by the interaction-regression term. Full assumptions, empirical-process definitions, and proofs are deferred to Appendix H.1, Appendix H.3, Appendix H.4, and Appendix H.5. The analysis treats the neural estimators as exact minimizers of the sample-split empirical squared-loss objectives defined in the appendix, with first-stage solutions plugged into the second-stage objectives. It establishes consistency in weak bridge norms [ 33 ]; optimization guarantees for the nonconvex stochastic training procedure, along the lines of Chen et al. [34] , are left for future work. Let m0(a, x, z ) := E[Y | A = a, X = x, Z = z] and r0(a, x, w ) := pA(a)/p A|X,W (a | x, w ). Define the conditional expectation operators Thh(a, x, z ) := E[h(a, x, W ) | A = a, X = x, Z = z] and Tφφ(a, x, w ) := 

E[φ(a, x, Z ) | A = a, X = x, W = w]. We measure bridge errors in the weak norms induced by Th and

, where ˆr is the fitted density-ratio target used to train TreatmentNet. We write Er :=

Theorem 5.1 gives double robustness in weak-norm form. The DRPCLNET-V1 estimator is consistent when the empirical outcome-bridge averaging in Eq. 4 and the DR stage-3 residual regression in Eq. 5 are consistent, and either the outcome-side weak-norm error Rweak  

> h

vanishes or the oracle treatment-side weak-norm error 

∥Tφ ˆφ−r0∥2  

> L2(PA,X,W )

vanishes. Equivalently, on the treatment side it suffices that Rweak ,ˆrφ +Er → 0, since ∥Tφ ˆφ−

r0∥2  

> L2(PA,X,W )

≤ 2Rweak ,ˆrφ +2 Er . Importantly, our result does not require controlling strong L2-norm convergence of either bh to h0 or bφ to φ0. Since the bridge functions h0 and φ0 are solutions to ill-posed inverse problems, controlling L2-norms would require restrictive assumptions on measure of ill-posedness [ 37 , 38 ]. Our result builds on weak norm rates given in Theorem H.2, Theorem H.11, and Theorem H.27. We achieve this by building on the doubly robust identification formula in Bozkurt et al. [27 , Lemma E.22 ] and Deaner [14 , Theorem 4.1 ].The corresponding consistency guarantees for heterogeneous and conditional dose-response estimation follow by the same weak-norm arguments and are given in Appendix H.6 and H.7, respectively. 

# 6 Numerical experiments 

We evaluate the proposed neural doubly robust proxy estimators against OutcomeNet, TreatmentNet, and applicable proxy baselines, including DRKPV [ 27 ], PKDR [ 26 ], and KPV [ 15 , 16 ]. Performance is measured by causal MSE between estimated and ground-truth response curves over the evaluation grid. Baselines are included only when compatible with the benchmark: DRKPV is omitted in the high-dimensional synthetic setting with observed X, PKDR is omitted for image-valued dSprites treatments, and CATE comparisons use KPV since doubly robust proxy baselines are not available for that target. Implementation details, architectures, hyperparameters, and density-ratio estimation procedures are given in Appendix I. 

Low-dimensional continuous dose-response. We first consider the low-dimensional continuous-treatment benchmark of Wu et al. [26] . This setting contains nonlinear latent confounding and noisy proxy measurements. The latent variables are U1 ∼ U [−1, 2] and U2 ∼ U [0 , 1] − 1{0 ≤ U1 ≤ 1}. The outcome and proxies are generated as W = ( U2 + U[−1, 1] , U 1 + N (0 , 1)) ⊤, Z = ( U2 + N (0 , 1) , U 1 + U[−1, 1]) ⊤, A = U1 + N (0 , 1) , and 

Y = 3 cos {2(0 .3U2 + 0 .3U1 + 0 .2) + 1 .5A} + N (0 , 1) .

High-dimensional synthetic dose-response. We next evaluate performance in the high-dimensional synthetic benchmark adapted from Singh [16] . In this setting, the observed covariates X, treatment proxy 

Z, and outcome proxy W are high-dimensional vectors, with unobserved confounding induced by latent noise components shared across the proxy, treatment, and outcome mechanisms. The continuous treatment assignment depends nonlinearly on summaries of X and Z, together with a latent confounding term. The outcome depends on the structural dose-response component fATE (a) = a2 + 1 .2a, summaries of X and W , a treatment-covariate interaction, and latent noise. Appendix I.2 gives the full data-generating process. 

Image-valued dSprites dose-response. We also consider a structured high-dimensional treatment bench-mark based on dSprites [ 39 , 17 ]. Here, each treatment A ∈ R4096 is a flattened 64 × 64 image, and the target 82000 5000 10000 15000 20000

DRKPV (Nystrom) denotes the low-rank approximation used for dSprites experiment as exact DRKPV exceeded memory limits for large sample sizes. response is determined by the image functional fATE (A) = {( vec (B)⊤A)2 − 3000 }/500 , where Bij = |32 − j|/32 .The outcome is confounded by the latent vertical position posY , with Y = 12( posY − 0.5) 2fATE (A) + N (0 , 0.52).The treatment proxy Z consists of the latent factors (scale , rotation , posX ), while the outcome proxy W is a separate image sharing the same posY .

Synthetic heterogeneous dose-response. To evaluate CATE estimation, we adapt the binary-treatment benchmark of Abrevaya et al. [40] to the proxy causal learning setting. We use V = ϵ0 as the covariate indexing heterogeneity, and define the latent confounders as U1 = 1 + 2 V + ϵ1, U2 = 1 + 2 V + ϵ2, and U3 = ( V − 1) 2 + ϵ3,where ϵi ∼ U [−0.5, 0.5] . The observed outcome is Y = V U 1U2U3 + ν when A = 1 and Y = 0 when A = 0 , with 

ν ∼ N (0 , 0.25 2). The ground-truth heterogeneous response at A = 1 is fCATE (1 , v ) = v(1+2 v)2(v −1) 2. Proxies are noisy measurements of the latent confounders: W1 = U1 +U[−b, b ], W2 = U2 +N (0 , σ 2), W3 = U3 +N (0 , σ 2),

Z1 = U1 + N (0 , σ 2), Z2 = U2 + U[−b, b ], and Z3 = U3 + U[−b, b ], with b = σ = 0 .1.Across the four benchmarks, DRPCLNET-V1 and DRPCLNET-V2 are consistently among the strongest performers, often improving over single-bridge neural estimators and kernel baselines as sample size increases. These results suggest combining learned outcome and treatment bridges stabilizes causal function estimation across low-dimensional and structured/high-dimensional settings. Additional ATT experiments, ablations, and proxy-misspecification analyses appear in Appendix I.4. 97 Conclusion 

Summary. We introduced a neural doubly robust framework for proxy causal learning with continuous and structured treatments. The method combines a neural outcome bridge with a new neural mean-embedding treatment bridge, and uses final regression stages to estimate population, heterogeneous, and conditional response functions. We also established weak-norm error bounds showing that the doubly robust error is controlled by the final averaging/regression errors and the smaller of the outcome- and treatment-side bridge errors. Empirically, the proposed estimators perform favorably against single-bridge neural estimators and existing proxy baselines across synthetic and image-valued benchmarks. As a broader impact, the method may support better causal analysis from imperfect observational data, but incorrect conclusions may affect downstream decisions if the proxy variables do not satisfy the assumptions needed for bridge identification. 

Limitations and future work. The neural estimators require more tuning than kernel or semiparametric baselines, and TreatmentNet depends on a precomputed density-ratio estimate whose error enters our bounds. Reducing hyperparameter sensitivity and analyzing the nonconvex stochastic optimization remain important future directions. 10 References

In this appendix, we prove the treatment-bridge identification result for the heterogeneous dose-response 

fCATE (a, v ) stated in Theorem 3.2. The corresponding ATE and ATT statements follow from Bozkurt et al. [21] . To match the notation of the main text, we write X = ( S, V ) and use p to denote densities or probability mass functions whenever they exist. 

Proof of the CATE statement in Theorem 3.2. Fix a ∈ A and v ∈ V , and write φ0 = φCATE 0 for brevity. By assumption, φ0 satisfies 

E[φ0(a, v, S, Z ) | A = a, S, V = v, W ] = pA|V (a | v)

pA|S,V,W (a | S, v, W ) PW |A=a,S,V =v -a.e. (6) Define

# B Doubly robust identification of causal functions: dose, heteroge-neous, and conditional response curves 

In this section, we prove Theorem 3.3. The key point is that each doubly robust formula remains valid if either the outcome bridge or the corresponding treatment bridge is correctly specified. 

Proof. We prove the result separately for the ATE, CATE, and ATT. Throughout, we use the notation 

X = ( S, V ), and we write h0(a, X, W ) for the outcome bridge. The ATE formula is the covariate adjusted version of Bozkurt et al. [27] [Theorem 2.7]. So, below, we prove CATE and ATT extensions. 

CATE. Consider

If h = h0, then 

f (DR) CATE (a, v ; h0, φ ) = E[φ(a, v, S, Z ) E[Y − h0(a, X, W ) | A = a, S, V = v, Z ]|A = a, V = v]+ E[h0(a, X, W ) | V = v]= E[h0(a, X, W ) | V = v] = fCATE (a, v ),

where the second equality again follows from Equation 1, since X = ( S, V ), and the last equality follows from Theorem 3.1. If φ = φCATE 0 , then 

E[φCATE 0 (a, v, S, Z )h(a, X, W ) | A = a, V = v]= EE[φCATE 0 (a, v, S, Z ) | A = a, S, V = v, W ] h(a, X, W ) A = a, V = v

f (DR) CATE (a, v ; h, φ CATE 0 ) = E[Y φ CATE 0 (a, v, S, Z ) | A = a, V = v]

− E[φCATE 0 (a, v, S, Z )h(a, X, W ) | A = a, V = v] + E[h(a, X, W ) | V = v]= E[Y φ CATE 0 (a, v, S, Z ) | A = a, V = v] = fCATE (a, v ),

where the last equality follows from Theorem 3.2. This proves the double robustness of all three identification formulas. 

# C Semiparametric efficiency theory and influence functions in the discrete setting 

In this section, we derive and formally establish the Efficient Influence Functions (EIFs) for the bridge-based target causal functions. We focus specifically on the discrete treatment setting, where A takes values in a finite set A. The restriction to discrete A is important: pointwise dose-response functionals are pathwise differentiable in the usual semiparametric sense for atoms A = a, whereas continuous-treatment pointwise effects require a different local or smoothed efficiency theory [ 41 , 42 , 26 , 43 ]. For the CATE result below we also take V to be discrete, so that P(V = v) > 0.Throughout this section, O = ( Y, A, W, Z, X ), and for CATE we write X = ( S, V ) and use h0(a, S, V, W ) as shorthand for h0(a, X, W ). Define 

πa := P(A = a), πv := P(V = v), πa|v := P(A = a | V = v).

All expectations are taken under the true observed law unless otherwise stated. 

Semiparametric regularity. In addition to the bridge existence, uniqueness, square-integrability, and positivity conditions stated below, we use the same tangent-space regularity condition as in [ 13 ] and [ 27 ]. Namely, the relevant conditional expectation operators are assumed to have dense range; the stronger surjectivity condition on the operators and their adjoints used by [ 13 ] is sufficient. For the ATE and ATT formulas, one may take 

T g := E[g(W, A, X ) | Z, A, X ], T ∗r := E[r(Z, A, X ) | W, A, X ],

with T : L2(PW,A,X ) → L2(PZ,A,X ). For the CATE formula, with X = ( S, V ), take 

TCg := E[g(W, A, S, V ) | Z, A, S, V ], T ∗

> C

r := E[r(Z, A, S, V ) | W, A, S, V ].

18 This condition is used only to conclude that the influence functions derived below are canonical gradients, hence efficient.

Remark C.2 (Relation to classical proximal ATE identification) . In the discrete-treatment case, our ATE representation is a normalized version of Cui et al. [13] . For a fixed treatment level a, their treatment bridge may be written as 

E[qATE 0 (a, X, Z ) | A = a, X, W ] = 1

p(A = a | X, W ) .

Their corresponding doubly robust representation of the counterfactual mean is 

f (DR) ATE (a; h0, q ATE 0 ) = E1{A = a}qATE 0 (a, X, Z ){Y − h0(A, X, W )} + E[h0(a, X, W )] . (20) 

Equivalently, its influence-function moment is 

ψEIF 

> qATE 0

(O; a) = 1{A = a}qATE 0 (a, X, Z ){Y − h0(A, X, W )} + h0(a, X, W ) − fATE (a).

Our bridge in Eq. 2 satisfies 

E[φATE 0 (a, X, Z ) | A = a, X, W ] = p(a)

p(A = a | X, W ) .

Thus, when the bridge solution is unique, φATE 0 (a, X, Z ) = p(a)qATE 0 (a, X, Z ). Substituting this into Eq. 20 gives 

EφATE 0 (a, X, Z ){Y − h0(a, X, W )} | A = a + E[h0(a, X, W )] ,

which is exactly Eq. 3. Therefore the normalized bridge does not change the discrete EIF; it rewrites the same moment as a conditional expectation given A = a. This form is the one used by our neural mean-embedding estimator and avoids an explicit sparse indicator moment when the target is indexed by a continuous or structured treatment value. 

Remark C.3 (Relation to the classical proximal ATT EIF) . The ATT formula above is not obtained by multiplying the whole ATE EIF by 1{A = a′}/π a′ . The bridge-residual term remains on the event {A = a},while the centering term is evaluated on the event {A = a′}.Consider the binary case a = 0 , a′ = 1 , and define μ0 := fATT (0 , 1) = E[Y (0) | A = 1] . Cui et al.’s ATT bridge is restricted to the control arm and satisfies 

E[hATT 0 (X, W ) | A = 0 , X, Z ] = E[Y | A = 0 , X, Z ],

E[qATT 0 (X, Z ) | A = 0 , X, W ] = p(A = 1 | X, W )

p(A = 0 | X, W ) .

1{A = 1 }

Hence ψEIF ATT (O; a, a ′) in Theorem C.1 is the EIF for the conditional counterfactual mean fATT (a, a ′); the EIF for an ATT contrast is obtained by adding the observed-arm component and taking the difference. 

Remark C.4 (Continuous and structured treatments) . The EIFs in Theorem C.1 are discrete-treatment EIFs. They justify the corresponding discrete doubly robust moment equations. The identification formulas in Theorem 3.3 are still stated for continuous or structured A, provided the bridge equations are interpreted through regular conditional laws and the required positivity conditions hold. However, the pointwise semiparametric efficiency theory for continuous treatments is not obtained by replacing 1{A = a}/p (a) with a density ratio. Existing continuous-treatment DR theory typically works with localized or smoothed target functionals [ 41 , 42 ,26 , 43 ]. Our contribution in the continuous and high-dimensional setting is therefore an identification and estimation construction based on normalized treatment bridges and neural mean embeddings, not a closed-form pointwise continuous-treatment EIF. 

Remark C.5 (Heterogeneous dose-response identification) . Equation 3 extends the doubly robust proximal identification formula from population dose-response curves to the heterogeneous response function fCATE (a, v ) = 

E[Y (a) | V = v]. We are not aware of a previous proximal doubly robust identification formula for the full curve 

(a, v ) 7 → fCATE (a, v ). This is distinct from the binary ATT case: the conditioning event is an observed effect modifier V = v, not the realized treatment arm. 

# D Review of existing multi-stage kernel algorithms with fixed feature maps 

We briefly review the kernel-based multi-stage estimators that serve as the main fixed-feature baselines for our method. In particular, the doubly robust kernel estimator DRKPV of Bozkurt et al. [27] combines two components: the outcome-bridge estimator KPV of Mastouri et al. [15] and the treatment-bridge estimator KAP of Bozkurt et al. [21] . Both methods are built in RKHSs with feature maps fixed by the choice of kernels. This makes them a natural starting point for our neural construction, where these fixed feature maps are replaced by adaptive learned representations. 

D.1 Kernel Proxy Variable: outcome-bridge method 

The Kernel Proxy Variable (KPV) method of Mastouri et al. [15] estimates the outcome bridge function 

h0 : A × X × W → R by solving Equation 1. In the RKHS formulation, one assumes that h0 ∈ H , where 

H = HA ⊗ H X ⊗ H W . Then the dose-response curve admits the representation 

fATE (a) = E[h0(a, X, W )] = ⟨h0, ϕ A(a) ⊗ μXW ⟩H , μXW := E[ϕX (X) ⊗ ϕW (W )] .

Replacing h0 and μXW by their empirical estimators yields

Dˆh, ϕ A(a) ⊗ ˆμXW

> i=1

The neural estimator introduced later keep the same multi-stage structure, but replace these fixed RKHS representations with adaptive feature maps learned from data [ 17 ]. We also note that Singh [16] provides a kernel implementation for heterogeneous and conditional treatment effects; in our experiments, we compare against this baseline using the implementation style of Mastouri et al. [15] together with the numerically more stable formulation of Xu and Gretton [47 , Appendix F ] , which is computationally easier to handle than the original variants. 

D.2 Kernel Alternative Proxy: treatment bridge-based method 

Complementing the outcome-bridge framework, Bozkurt et al. [21] introduced the Kernel Alternative Proxy (KAP) algorithm for treatment-bridge estimation. Since the original closed-form derivation is developed in the setting without additional observed covariates, we review that version here. Our neural treatment-bridge formulation in Section E.2 will later extend the same idea to the case with observed covariates X.In the original KAP formulation, the treatment bridge φ0 ∈ H A ⊗ H Z is defined by 

E[φ0(a, Z ) | A = a, W ] = pW (W )pA(a)

pW,A (W, a ) .

Given such a bridge, the dose-response is identified by 

fATE (a) = E[Y φ 0(a, Z ) | A = a],

as stated in Theorem 3.2 in the no-X setting. 24 To estimate φ0, KAP considers the regularized squared loss 

Lφ, 2(φ) = E

Lφ, 2(φ) = EE[φ(A, Z ) | W, A ]2 − 2 EW EA[E[φ(A, Z ) | W, A ]] + λφ, 2∥φ∥2

E[Y ˆφ(a, Z ) | A = a] = ⟨ ˆφ, ϕ A(a) ⊗ E[Y ϕ Z (Z) | A = a]⟩ . (27) The term μY Z |A(a) := E[Y ϕ Z (Z) | A = a] is estimated by vector-valued ridge regression, leading to a fully closed-form estimator. Using the notation of Bozkurt et al. [21] , the final estimator can be written as 

ˆfATE (a) = α⊤E(a), where E(a) is assembled from the first- and third-stage kernel matrices; the explicit expression is given in Algorithm 4.1 of Bozkurt et al. [21]. As in KPV, the main limitation for our purposes is that all feature maps are fixed once the kernels are chosen. Our neural treatment-bridge estimator keeps the same multi-stage logic, but replaces these fixed RKHS representations by adaptive learned features. 25 D.3 Doubly Robust Kernel Proxy Variable 

The Doubly Robust Kernel Proxy Variable (DRKPV) estimator of Bozkurt et al. [27] combines the kernel outcome-bridge estimator KPV with the kernel treatment-bridge estimator KAP. In the notation of the previous two subsections, it targets the doubly robust representation 

f (DR) ATE (a) = E[φ0(a, Z ){Y − h0(a, W )} | A = a] + E[h0(a, W )] .

Thus, the estimator consists of three parts: an outcome-bridge term E[h0(a, W )] , a treatment-bridge term 

E[Y φ 0(a, Z ) | A = a], and a correction term E[φ0(a, Z )h0(a, W ) | A = a]. The first two are obtained from KPV and KAP, respectively. The only additional ingredient is therefore the cross term E[φ0(a, Z )h0(a, W ) | A = a]. Following Bozkurt et al. [27], this quantity is approximated by plugging in the learned bridge functions and writing 

E[ ˆ φ(a, Z )ˆh(a, W ) | A = a] = 

D

ˆφ ⊗ ˆh, μ ZW |A(a) ⊗ ϕA(a) ⊗ ϕA(a)

E

,

where μZW |A(a) := E[ϕZ (Z) ⊗ ϕW (W ) | A = a]. This conditional mean embedding is estimated by a further vector-valued ridge regression on a third split of the data. If ξi(a) = (KAA + tλ DR I)−1KAa

> i

, then the resulting closed-form approximation of the cross term is 

E[ ˆ φ(a, Z )ˆh(a, W ) | A = a] ≈

> t

> i=1

ξi(a) ˆ φ(a, z i)ˆh(a, w i).

Putting the pieces together, DRKPV takes the form 

ˆf (DR) ATE (a) = ˆf (h)ATE (a) + ˆf (φ)ATE (a) − ˆf (cross) ATE (a),

where ˆf (h)ATE (a) is the KPV estimate, ˆf (φ)ATE (a) is the KAP estimate, and ˆf (cross) ATE (a) is the conditional mean embedding approximation above. By construction, this estimator inherits the doubly robust identification property: it remains valid if either the outcome bridge or the treatment bridge is correctly specified. As with KPV and KAP, however, all feature maps are fixed by the choice of kernels, which is precisely the limitation addressed by our neural extension. 

# E Neural mean embedding-based proxy causal learning for dose-response 

In this sextion, we elaborate the algorithmic structure of the Neural Mean Embedding (NME) framework for dose-response estimation. The guiding idea is to preserve the multi-stage bridge-based structure induced by the identification strategy, while replacing fixed RKHS feature maps by trainable neural representations. This allows the representation itself to adapt to the data and makes the resulting estimators suitable for stochastic optimization in high-dimensional and structured settings. Our construction consists of three components: 1. Outcome bridge network. We begin with the Deep Feature Proxy Causal Learning (DFPCL) architecture of Xu et al. [17] , which provides a neural parameterization of the outcome bridge estimator. We refine this component by incorporating proximal closed-form updates for the final linear layer and by introducing a hybrid optimization scheme for the second-stage head. This latter modification allows the use of general robust regression losses, such as the Huber loss, rather than restricting the training objective to mean squared error. 2. Treatment bridge network. We next introduce a neural estimator of the treatment bridge function in Section E.2. This construction follows the same principles as the outcome-side network: adaptive feature learning, proximal closed-form updates for the last linear layer, and hybrid optimization in the second stage. 26 3. Neural doubly robust unification. Finally, in Section E.3, we combine the outcome- and treatment-bridge components into a fully neural doubly robust estimator of the dose-response curve. This final stage is designed to leverage the complementary strengths of both bridge functions. 

E.1 Dose-response curve estimation: outcome bridge-based approach 

We first describe the outcome-bridge component, which builds on the DFPCL architecture of Xu et al. [17] .We refer to this component as OutcomeNet . To distinguish it from the treatment-side construction introduced later in Section E.2, we attach the superscript (h) to all quantities associated with the outcome bridge. Let D = {(ai, y i, w i, z i, x i)}Ni=1 be an observational dataset of size N . As in the fixed-feature multi-stage estimators, the learning procedure is split into two stages. The first stage learns a neural conditional mean embedding of the outcome proxy, while the second stage learns the outcome bridge itself. To reflect this structure, we partition D into two subsets, 

D(h)1 = {(¯ ai, ¯yi, ¯wi, ¯zi, ¯xi)}nh

> i=1

> i=1

,

where D(h)1 is used in the first stage and D(h)2 in the second stage. Depending on the implementation, these two subsets may be disjoint or partially overlapping. The neural parameterization mirrors the two-stage bridge structure reviewed in the kernel setting. The outcome bridge function is modeled as

• h is the second-stage linear head mapping the tensorized representation of (A, X, W ) to the scalar bridge value h(a, x, w ).This parameterization separates the two roles played by the neural model. The first stage learns how the information contained in (A, X, Z ) predicts the proxy representation of W . The second stage then uses this learned proxy representation to fit the outcome bridge. In this way, the architecture preserves the logic of proximal bridge estimation while allowing the representation spaces themselves to be adapted during training. OutcomeNet is trained in two stages. The first stage learns a neural conditional mean embedding of the outcome proxy, and the second stage uses this learned representation to estimate the outcome bridge function. As in the fixed-feature setting, this two-stage structure is dictated by the bridge equation itself. The difference is that, in the neural setting, both the feature maps and the final linear layers are learned from data. The first stage estimates the neural conditional mean embedding

Proximal closed-form updates. The analytical updates in Equations 32 and 33 are exact minimizers of the corresponding quadratic objectives on a fixed dataset. In stochastic mini-batch training, however, directly replacing the last-layer parameters by these batchwise optima can lead to unstable trajectories, since each batch induces a different local optimum. Following Galashov et al. [28] , we therefore replace the zero-centered ridge penalties by proximal penalties centered at the previous iterates. This keeps the batchwise closed-form updates close to the current parameter state and substantially stabilizes training. In the present two-stage setting, there is one additional subtlety. The stage-2 loss depends on the first-stage optimum through the predicted proxy features. Consequently, in addition to the persistent first-stage iterate

is treated as fixed, exactly as in the non-proximal DFPCL update. 

Flexible second-stage optimization with robust loss functions. The proximal update in Equation 41 is available only for a quadratic second-stage loss. To allow greater flexibility, we replace the squared loss in the second stage by a general differentiable regression loss ℓh, 2(·, ·). In particular, this allows the use of robust losses such as the Huber loss [31], which can be less sensitive to outliers than mean squared error. To define the resulting objective, recall that the stage-2 prediction uses the auxiliary first-stage operator computed on the current second-stage batch. The generalized second-stage batch loss is then 

ˆLgen 

> h, 2



θ(h)2 , h; θ(h)1 , B(h)2



= 1

|B (h)2 |

X

> i∈B (h)2

ℓh, 2



˜yi, h⊤Ψ(h)2,i 



+ λ(h)2 ∥h − ˆht∥22. (42) Once the loss is no longer quadratic, the exact proximal closed-form update for h is no longer available. We therefore use a two-step strategy inside each stage-2 iteration. 1. Featurizer update. We first update the second-stage neural parameters θ(h)2 by a gradient step on Equation 42, using the current estimate of the head and the current auxiliary first-stage operator ˇV (h) 

> t

.2. Head refinement. Holding the feature extractors fixed, we then refine the second-stage linear head h by approximately minimizing Equation 42 with respect to h. In our implementation, this inner optimization is performed by Kh steps of L-BFGS [32], using the PyTorch implementation [49]. This numerical refinement plays the same role as the closed-form update in the quadratic case: it keeps the final linear layer close to the minimizer of the current second-stage objective. When ℓh, 2 is chosen to be the squared loss and the inner optimization is run to convergence, the resulting update recovers the proximal quadratic solution in Equation 41. The advantage of Equation 42 is therefore not conceptual but practical: it preserves the same multi-stage bridge-learning structure while allowing robust second-stage losses. E.2 Dose-response curve estimation: treatment bridge-based approach 

We now introduce the treatment bridge-based component of our neural mean embedding framework, which we refer to as TreatmentNet . In contrast to the kernel KAP derivation reviewed earlier, the present neural formulation explicitly incorporates the observed covariates X. The target of this component is the treatment bridge φ0(a, x, z ), which satisfies the conditional moment relation 

E[φ0(a, X, Z ) | A = a, X, W ] = r(a, X, W ), r(a, x, w ) := pA(a) pX,W (x, w )

pA,X,W (a, x, w ) .

Accordingly, we view treatment-bridge learning as a regression problem in which the unknown conditional expectation E[φ(A, X, Z ) | A, X, W ] is fitted to the density-ratio target r(A, X, W ).In practice, the ratio r(A, X, W ) is not known and is replaced by a pre-computed estimator ˆr(A, X, W ); see Appendix I.1 for details. E.3 Dose-response curve estimation: doubly robust approach 

We now combine the learned outcome and treatment bridges into a doubly robust estimator of the dose-response curve. Recall the identifying representation 

f (DR) ATE (a; h0, φ 0) = E[φ0(a, X, Z ){Y − h0(a, X, W )} | A = a] + E[h0(a, X, W )] .

Given estimators ˆh from OutcomeNet and ˆφ from TreatmentNet, the only remaining task is to estimate the conditional expectation terms appearing in this formula. We describe two implementations of this final stage. The first one is the version used in our main experiments. Let D(κ) = {(ai, y i, w i, z i, x i)}nκ 

> i=1

be a sample on which both ˆh and ˆφ can be evaluated. In our implementa-tion, we reuse the second-stage split for this purpose, although a separate split could also be used. 

Version 1: direct residual regression. The most direct implementation is to regress the bridge-weighted residual 

ˆφ(a, X, Z ){Y − ˆh(a, X, W )}

on the treatment value A. We therefore construct the pseudo-outcome dataset D(κ)1 = {(ai, y (κ, 1)

# F Neural mean embedding-based proxy causal learning for heteroge-neous dose-response 

In this section, we derive the doubly robust algorithm for heterogeneous dose-response curve. In particular, similar to dose-response counterpart, we firs derive the outcome bridge-based algorithm. Then, we derive the analogous treatment bridge algorithm, and then combine both into doubly robust algorithm. 

F.1 Heterogeneous dose-response estimation: outcome bridge method 

Throughout this subsection, we write X = ( S, V ), where V denotes the conditioning variable and S collects the remaining observed covariates. Recall that the target is given by fCATE (a, v ) = E[h0(a, v, S, W ) | V = v].

According to Theorem 3.1, the corresponding outcome bridge must satisfy 

E[h0(a, v, S, W ) | A = a, V = v, S, Z ] = E[Y | A = a, V = v, S, Z ].

Thus, the overall learning procedure remains the same as in Appendix E.1: the first stage learns a neural conditional mean embedding of the outcome proxy, and the second stage learns the outcome bridge itself. The only structural change is that the heterogeneity variable V is now included explicitly in both stages. Let D(h)1 = {( ¯ai, ¯vi, ¯si, ¯zi, ¯wi)}nh

> i=1

, D(h)2 = {( ˜ai, ˜vi, ˜si, ˜zi, ˜wi, ˜yi)}mh 

> i=1

F.2 Heterogeneous dose-response estimation: treatment bridge method 

We now extend TreatmentNet to the heterogeneous dose-response function. As in the outcome-bridge subsection, we write X = ( S, V ), where V denotes the effect modifiers of interest and S collects the remaining observed covariates. Recall that, according to Theorem 3.2, the heterogeneous dose-response is identified through the treatment bridge by 

fCATE (a, v ) = E[Y φ 0(a, v, S, Z ) | A = a, V = v],

where the corresponding bridge function satisfies 

E[φ0(a, v, S, Z ) | A = a, V = v, S, W ] = pA|V (a | v)

pA|S,V,W (a | s, v, w ) .

Thus, compared with the population-level treatment-bridge formulation, the only structural change is again the explicit inclusion of V in the bridge and in the neural feature maps. In practice, the conditional density ratio is not known and is replaced by an estimator ˆr(a, v, s, w ) ≈

pA|V (a | v)/p A|S,V,W (a | s, v, w ); see Appendix I.1. Let D(φ)1 = {( ¯ai, ¯vi, ¯si, ¯wi, ¯zi)}nφ

F.3 Heterogeneous dose-response estimation: doubly robust method 

Having obtained heterogeneous bridge estimators from Appendices F.1 and F.2, we now combine them into a doubly robust estimator of the heterogeneous dose-response curve. Recall the identifying representation 

f (DR) CATE (a, v ; h0, φ 0) = E[φ0(a, v, S, Z ){Y − h0(a, v, S, W )} | A = a, V = v]+ E[h0(a, v, S, W ) | V = v].

Given estimators ˆh and ˆφ, the remaining task is therefore to estimate the conditional expectation terms appearing in this expression over the joint space (A, V ). As in the population-level case, we describe two implementations of this final stage. Let D(κ) = {(ai, v i, s i, w i, z i, y i)}nκ 

> i=1

be a sample on which both ˆh and ˆφ

can be evaluated. In our implementation, we reuse the second-stage split for this purpose, although a separate split may also be used. 

Version 1: direct residual regression. The most direct implementation is to regress the bridge-weighted residual 

ˆφ(a, v, S, Z ){Y − ˆh(a, v, S, W )}

on the pair (A, V ). We therefore construct the pseudo-outcome dataset 

D(κ)1 = {(( ai, v i), y (κ, 1)

k(κ, 1) (a, v ; θ(κ)1 ) ≈ E[ ˆ φ(a, v, S, Z ){Y − ˆh(a, v, S, W )} | A = a, V = v].

More generally, this network may be trained with any differentiable regression loss. Writing ℓ(κ)1 for this loss, the third-stage objective is

f (DR) CATE (a, v ; h0, φ 0) = E[h0(a, v, S, W ) | V = v] + E[Y φ 0(a, v, S, Z ) | A = a, V = v]

− E[φ0(a, v, S, Z )h0(a, v, S, W ) | A = a, V = v].

# G Neural mean embedding-based proxy causal learning for condi-tional dose-response 

We now extend the neural mean embedding framework to the conditional dose-response function fATT (a, a ′).As in the previous sections, we proceed by developing corresponding outcome- and treatment-bridge estimators and then combining them into a doubly robust construction. We begin with the outcome-bridge formulation. In this case, the bridge-learning problem itself is unchanged relative to the population-level dose-response setting; the only modification appears in the final integration step, which now conditions on the observed treatment level A = a′.

G.1 Conditional dose-response estimation: outcome bridge method 

Recall that, according to Theorem 3.1, the conditional dose-response is identified by fATT (a, a ′) =

E[h0(a, X, W ) | A = a′]. The bridge equation remains exactly the same as in the population-level outcome-bridge formulation. Therefore, the first two stages of the estimator are identical to those of OutcomeNet in Appendix E.1. In particular, we reuse the same neural parameterization of the bridge function h(a, x, w ) and the same 42 two-stage training procedure to obtain an estimator ˆh.The only new ingredient is the final conditional integration step. Using Equation 28, we have 

fATT (a, a ′) ≈ E[ˆh(a, X, W ) | A = a′]= E

G.2 Conditional dose-response estimation: treatment bridge method 

We now extend TreatmentNet to the conditional dose-response function fATT (a, a ′). For a fixed reference treatment level a′, Theorem 3.2 gives the identifying representation 

fATT (a, a ′) = E[Y φ 0(a, a ′, X, Z ) | A = a],

where the corresponding treatment bridge satisfies 

E[φ0(a, a ′, X, Z ) | A = a, X, W ] = pX,W |A(X, W | a′)

pX,W |A(X, W | a) .

Thus, in contrast to the population-level treatment-bridge setting, the bridge now depends on the additional reference level a′. In the implementation considered here, we treat a′ as fixed and learn a separate treatment bridge for each reference level of interest. 43 An important simplification is that the first-stage conditional mean embedding of the treatment proxy does not depend on a′. G.3 Conditional dose-response estimation: doubly robust method 

We now combine the conditional outcome- and treatment-bridge estimators into a doubly robust estimator of the conditional dose-response function. Recall that, for a fixed reference treatment level a′, the identifying representation is 

f (DR) ATT (a, a ′; h0, φ 0) = E[φ0(a, a ′, X, Z ){Y − h0(a, X, W )} | A = a] + E[h0(a, X, W ) | A = a′].

Thus, given estimators ˆh and ˆφ(a′), the remaining task is to estimate the conditional expectation terms appearing in this expression as functions of the intervention level a. As in the population-level and heterogeneous settings, we describe two implementations of this final stage. Let D(κ,a ′) = {(ai, x i, w i, z i, y i)}nκ 

> i=1

denote a sample on which both ˆh and ˆφ(a′) can be evaluated. In our implementation, we reuse the second-stage split for this purpose, although one may alternatively use a separate split. 45 Version 1: direct residual regression. For fixed a′, the most direct implementation is to regress the bridge-weighted residual 

ˆφ(a′)(a, X, Z ){Y − ˆh(a, X, W )}

on the treatment level A. We therefore construct the pseudo-outcome dataset D(κ,a ′)1 = {(ai, y (κ, 1,a ′)

k(κ, 1,a ′)(a; θ(κ,a ′)1 ) ≈ E[ ˆ φ(a′)(a, X, Z ){Y − ˆh(a, X, W )} | A = a].

More generally, this network may be trained with any differentiable regression loss. Writing ℓ(κ, ATT) 1 for this loss, the third-stage objective is

− E[φ0(a, a ′, X, Z )h0(a, X, W ) | A = a].

# H Consistency of the proposed algorithms 

In this section, we prove the consistency results of our proposed algorithms. For simplicity of the analysis, we assume independent splits of the dataset for each regression stage. 

H.1 Outcome bridge consistency for dose-response estimation 

We derive weak norm convergence rates for the outcome bridge in this subsection. Our proof relies on uniform deviation inequalities and controlling the Rademacher complexities of relevant function classes. Let 

Q := ( A, X, Z ), R := ( A, X ),

and write 

m0(q) := E[Y | Q = q].

Corollary H.3 (Weak-norm consistency) . Suppose 

κ1,ν h → 0, κ2,ν h → 0,

and 

bRD(h)1

(L1,ν h ) → 0, bRD(h)2

(L2,ν h ) → 0

in probability, with nh, m h → ∞ . Then 

∥Thˆh − m0∥2 

> L2(PQ)

→ 0

in probability. If an outcome bridge h0 exists with Thh0 = m0, then 

∥ˆh − h0∥2 

> Th

→ 0

in probability. 

Remark H.4 (What this result does and does not prove) . This theorem establishes convergence rates for the outcome bridge function in the weak norm induced by the operator Th

∥ˆh − h0∥Th = E[ˆh(A, X, W ) − h0(A, X, W ) | A, X, Z ] L2(PA,X,Z )

.

52 It does not imply 

∥ˆh − h0∥L2(PA,X,W ) → 0,

in general. Indeed, since h0 is the solution to an ill-posed inverse problem, obtaining strong norm convergence for ˆh requires controlling sieve measure of ill-posedness, which generally diverges as sieve dimension grows [ 37 , 50 , 38 , 51 , 52 , 53 ]. In the severely ill-posed case, strong-norm convergence can be arbitrarily slow. The result below avoids this difficulty for the outcome-bridge plug-in estimator: using the dual treatment bridge, the dose-response functional is well-posed with respect to the weak residual Thˆh − m0, so projected convergence is sufficient for consistency [14]. This weak-norm control is then used later in the doubly robust analysis. 

Remark H.5 (Approximate optimization) . If the first-stage and second-stage empirical minimizations are solved only up to optimization errors η1,ν h and η2,ν h , respectively, the same proof goes through with additional additive terms of order 

B2 

> θ

η1,ν h + η2,ν h .

The theorem above sets these errors to zero because it analyzes the exact profile-ERM idealization, not the stochastic nonconvex optimizer.

H.3 Treatment bridge consistency for dose-response estimation 

We obtain convergence rates for TreatmentNet by controlling the Rademacher complexity of relevant function classes. Our proof is the counterpart of the corresponding analysis for OutcomeNet in Appendix H.1. Remark H.14 (What this result does and does not prove) . This theorem proves convergence only in the projected treatment-bridge seminorm: 

∥ ˆφ − φ0∥Tφ = ∥E[ ˆ φ(A, X, Z ) − φ0(A, X, Z ) | A, X, W ]∥L2(PA,X,W ) .

It does not imply 

∥ ˆφ − φ0∥L2(PA,X,Z ) → 0

without an additional inverse-stability assumption. No strong bridge convergence is used in the dose-response result below. 

Remark H.15 (Role of density-ratio estimation) . The density-ratio nuisance enters only through 

Er = ∥ˆr − r0∥2 

> L2(PQφ )

.

The treatment bridge is trained against the plug-in target ˆr, while the causal target is defined by r0. Corollary H.12 separates these two errors explicitly. Establishing rates for Er depends on the chosen density-ratio estimator and is treated as an external nuisance problem. 

H.4 Dose-response consistency of TreatmentNet 

We now convert the projected treatment-bridge result into consistency of the ATE dose-response curve. This step does not require strong convergence of ˆφ in L2(PA,X,Z ). Instead, it uses the outcome bridge as the dual object and controls the population dose-response error through the projected treatment-bridge residual. Recall that 

Qφ := ( A, X, W ), r0(a, x, w ) = pA(a)

pA|X,W (a | x, w ) .

The treatment-bridge representation of the ATE dose-response curve is 

fATE (a) = E[Y φ 0(a, X, Z ) | A = a],

where 

Tφφ0 = r0.

¯f (φ)ˆφ (a) := E[Y ˆφ(a, X, Z ) | A = a].

H.5 Doubly robust dose-response consistency 

We now analyze DRPCLNET-V1 for the ATE dose-response curve. The bridge estimators ˆh and ˆφ, as well as the plug-in density-ratio estimator ˆr, are trained on samples independent of the final-stage residual-regression sample and are treated as fixed in the final-stage analysis. The result uses only projected bridge residuals. It does not require strong L2-convergence of either bridge. Let 

(Thh)( a, x, z ) := E[h(a, x, W ) | A = a, X = x, Z = z],

and 

(Tφφ)( a, x, w ) := E[φ(a, x, Z ) | A = a, X = x, W = w].

Write 

m0(a, x, z ) := E[Y | A = a, X = x, Z = z], r0(a, x, w ) := pA(a)

pA|X,W (a | x, w ) .

63 The oracle outcome and treatment bridge equations are 

Thh0 = m0, Tφφ0 = r0.

The density-ratio nuisance error is 

Er := ∥ˆr − r0∥2 

> L2(PA,X,W )

.

Population plug-in DR functional. Define 

μˆh(a) := E[ˆh(a, X, W )] 

and 

κˆh, ˆφ(a) := E[ ˆ φ(a, X, Z ){Y − ˆh(a, X, W )} | A = a].

The population plug-in DRPCLNET-V1 functional is 

¯f DR1 ATE (a) := μˆh(a) + κˆh, ˆφ(a).

Lemma H.23 (ATE doubly robust identity) . For every a,

fATE (a) − ¯f DR1 ATE (a) = E[{φ0(a, X, Z ) − ˆφ(a, X, Z )}{ h0(a, X, W ) − ˆh(a, X, W )} | A = a].

Proof. By the outcome bridge equation, 

E[Y − h0(a, X, W ) | A = a, X, Z ] = 0 .

Since ˆφ(a, X, Z ) is a function of (X, Z ),

E[ ˆ φ(a, X, Z ){Y − h0(a, X, W )} | A = a] = 0 .

Therefore, 

¯f DR1 ATE (a) = E[ˆh(a, X, W )] + E[ ˆ φ(a, X, Z ){h0(a, X, W ) − ˆh(a, X, W )} | A = a].

By the outcome bridge representation, 

fATE (a) = E[h0(a, X, W )] .

Hence 

¯f DR1 ATE (a) − fATE (a) = E[ˆh(a, X, W ) − h0(a, X, W )] + E[ ˆ φ(a, X, Z ){h0(a, X, W ) − ˆh(a, X, W )} | A = a].

The treatment bridge reweighting identity gives, for any square-integrable q(a, X, W ),

E[φ0(a, X, Z )q(a, X, W ) | A = a] = E[q(a, X, W )] .

Applying this identity with 

q(a, X, W ) = ˆh(a, X, W ) − h0(a, X, W )

yields 

E[ˆh(a, X, W ) − h0(a, X, W )] = E[φ0(a, X, Z ){ˆh(a, X, W ) − h0(a, X, W )} | A = a].

Substitution gives 

¯f DR1 ATE (a) − fATE (a) = E[{ ˆφ(a, X, Z ) − φ0(a, X, Z )}{ h0(a, X, W ) − ˆh(a, X, W )} | A = a].

Multiplying both sides by −1 proves the claim. 64 Projected residual bounds for the population DR remainder. Define the conditional bridge-error constants

Proof. Decompose

The residual-regression term is bounded by Lemma H.26. The population DR remainder is bounded by Lemma H.24. Combining these bounds gives the theorem. 

Corollary H.28 (Projected doubly robust consistency) . Suppose 

Eμ,h → 0, κDR ,ν DR → 0, ∆DR ,ν DR (δ) → 0.

If either the outcome-side projected residual vanishes, 

∥Thˆh − m0∥2  

> L2(PA,X,Z )

→ 0,

or the treatment-side plug-in projected residual and density-ratio error vanish, 

∥Tφ ˆφ − ˆr∥2  

> L2(PA,X,W )

→ 0, ∥ˆr − r0∥2  

> L2(PA,X,W )

→ 0,

then 

∥ ˆf DR1 ATE − fATE ∥L2(PA) → 0

in probability. 

Remark H.29 (Projected double robustness) . The theorem gives double robustness in projected-residual form. The estimator is consistent if either the outcome-side projected residual 

Thˆh − m0

vanishes, or the treatment-side projected residual 

Tφ ˆφ − ˆr

67 vanishes together with the density-ratio nuisance error ∥ˆr − r0∥2 

> L2(PA,X,W )

. No strong L2-convergence of either bridge is required. A second-order product rate in strong bridge errors would require additional strong-norm or conditional-profile assumptions and is not used here. 

H.6 Doubly robust heterogeneous dose-response consistency 

We now state the consistency results for the heterogeneous dose-response target 

fCATE (a, v ) := E[Y (a) | V = v].

Write X = ( S, V ), where V indexes the heterogeneity of interest and S collects the remaining observed covariates. All CATE errors are measured in L2(PA,V ). The analysis is the CATE analogue of the projected dose-response analysis in Appendix H.1 and Appendix H.3. Thus we only introduce the additional CATE notation and state the resulting bounds.

H.7 Doubly robust conditional dose-response consistency 

We now state the consistency results for the conditional dose-response target 

fATT (a, a ′) := E[Y (a) | A = a′].

Throughout this subsection, the anchor a′ is fixed. All errors are therefore pointwise in a′ and measured in 

L2(PA) over the intervention argument a. Uniformity over a′ would require the constants and third-stage regression errors below to be controlled uniformly over anchors. The ATT analysis is the fixed-anchor analogue of the projected dose-response analysis in Appendix H.1, Appendix H.3, and Appendix H.5. We therefore only introduce the ATT-specific notation and state the resulting bounds. 71 Projected bridge residuals. The outcome bridge is the same as in the dose-response case. Let

# I Supplementary on numerical experiments 

This appendix provides the experimental details omitted from the main text, including density-ratio estimation procedures, benchmark data-generating processes, implementation choices, hyperparameters, and additional results. 

I.1 Density ratio estimation algorithms used in simulations 

In this section, we summarize the density-ratio estimators used in our experiments. For population-level dose-response estimation, the treatment bridge requires the ratio 

rATE (a, x, w ) = p(a)

p(a | x, w ) = p(a) p(x, w )

p(a, x, w ) .

For heterogeneous dose-response estimation, the corresponding ratio is 

rCATE (a, v, s, w ) = p(a | v)

p(a | v, s, w ) = p(a, v ) p(v, s, w )

p(a, v, s, w ) p(v) .

We considered three estimation strategies, chosen according to the dimension and structure of the benchmark. 

Kernel density estimation. For the low-dimensional synthetic dose-response benchmark and the synthetic heterogeneous benchmark, we estimate the required density ratios by kernel density estimation [ 55 ]. In the population-level setting, we separately estimate the marginal density of A, the marginal density of (X, W ), and the joint density of (A, X, W ), and then form 

ˆrATE (a, x, w ) = ˆp(a) ˆ p(x, w )ˆp(a, x, w ) .

In the heterogeneous setting, we analogously estimate the four factors p(v), p(a, v ), p(v, s, w ), and p(a, v, s, w ),and define 

ˆrCATE (a, v, s, w ) = ˆp(a, v ) ˆ p(v, s, w )ˆp(a, v, s, w ) ˆ p(v) .

Our implementation uses Gaussian kernels and selects the bandwidth of each density estimator by a simple hold-out likelihood criterion over a log-spaced grid. For numerical stability, densities are evaluated in the log domain. 

KLIEP. For the dSprite benchmark, direct density-ratio estimation was more stable than separately estimating the numerator and denominator densities. We therefore use the Kullback–Leibler Importance Estimation Procedure (KLIEP) [ 56 ], which estimates the ratio directly without separately estimating the constituent densities. Since the treatment and outcome proxy are image-valued, we first compress them using a convolutional 

β-VAE [ 57 ]. In our implementation, the latent dimension is set to 16 and the target coefficient is β = 1 .0. We use the encoder mean μ as the learned low-dimensional representation. KLIEP is then applied in this latent space rather than on the raw 4096-dimensional images. 75 Concretely, we form numerator samples from the product-of-marginals construction ( eA, W ), where eA is obtained by randomly permuting the treatment samples, and denominator samples from the observed joint 

(A, W ). KLIEP models the ratio by a nonnegative Gaussian kernel expansion 

wα(u) = 

> b

X

> ℓ=1

αℓKσ (u, c ℓ), αℓ ≥ 0,

and selects the coefficients by maximizing the empirical log-likelihood on numerator samples subject to a normalization constraint on denominator samples. Kernel widths and the number of kernel centers are selected by the likelihood cross-validation procedure of Sugiyama et al. [56]. 

Conditional normalizing flows. For the high-dimensional synthetic dose-response benchmark, we estimate the population-level density ratio using conditional normalizing flows. Concretely, we fit one flow to the marginal density p(a) and a second flow to the conditional density p(a | x, w ), and then define 

ˆrATE (a, x, w ) = exp  log ˆ p(a) − log ˆ p(a | x, w ).

This avoids direct nonparametric density estimation in a regime where KDE is less reliable. Our implementation uses a train/validation split for model fitting and monitors the negative log-likelihood on the validation set. The normalizing-flow family is implemented through conditional flows, with the context given by (X, W ). We used this approach only in the high-dimensional synthetic experiment. 

ATT ratio from the ATE ratio. For conditional dose-response estimation with a fixed anchor treatment a′,we do not fit a separate density-ratio model. Instead, we reuse the estimator of rATE and obtain the required ATT ratio algebraically. In the setting with observed covariates X, the treatment bridge for ATT involves 

rATT (a, a ′, x, w ) := p(x, w | a′)

p(x, w | a) .

Using Bayes’ rule together with the definition of rATE , we obtain 

rATT (a, a ′, x, w ) = p(x, w | a′)

p(x, w | a) = p(a) p(a′, x, w )

p(a′) p(a, x, w ) = rATE (a, x, w )

rATE (a′, x, w ) .

Therefore, once ˆrATE has been fitted, the ATT ratio is obtained by evaluating the same estimator at the observed treatment a and at a′, and then taking their ratio. In the setting without additional covariates X, the same identity reduces to 

rATT (a, a ′, w ) = p(w | a′)

p(w | a) = rATE (a, w )

rATE (a′, w ) .

This is computationally convenient in practice, since changing the variable a′ only requires reevaluating the fitted ATE density-ratio estimator rather than retraining it. 

Experiment-specific choices. In summary, we use KDE for the low-dimensional synthetic dose-response and synthetic heterogeneous benchmarks, KLIEP with β-VAE compression for the dSprite benchmark, and conditional normalizing flows for the high-dimensional synthetic benchmark. 

I.2 Detailed data generating processes 

This section provides additional formal specifications for benchmarks whose full details are not included in the main text. 

High-dimensional benchmark. We adapt the high-dimensional proximal setting from Singh [16] . Each instance is generated through the following sequence: 

• Unobserved Noise: We draw {ϵi}3 

> i=1

∼ N (0 , 1) and high-dimensional noise vectors νz ∼ U [−1, 1] dz ,

νw ∼ U [−1, 1] dw , where dz and dw are the dimensions of the proxies. 76 • Latent Confounding: The unobserved confounders are defined as Uz = ϵ1 + ϵ3 and Uw = ϵ2 + ϵ3.

• Proxies: The proxy variables are constructed by injecting the latent signal into the noise vectors: 

Z = νz + 0 .25 Uz 1dz and W = νw + 0 .25 Uw1dw .

• Observed Covariates: X ∼ N (0 , Σ), where Σ is a tridiagonal covariance matrix with Σii = 1 and 

Σij = 0 .5 for |i − j| = 1 .

• Treatment mechanism: The continuous treatment is assigned as 

A = Λ 



3 X⊤1dx

√dx

+ 3 Z⊤1dz

√dz



+ 0 .25 Uw,

where Λ( t) = 0 .8 exp (t)/(1 + exp (t)) + 0 .1 is the truncated logistic link, mapping the linear signal into 

(0 .1, 0.9) . The normalization by 1/√d keeps the scale of the linear terms comparable across dimensions. 

• Outcome mechanism: The outcome is generated as 

Y = θATE (A) + 1 .2

 X⊤1dx

√dx

+ W ⊤1dw

√dw



+ AX 1 + 0 .25 Uz ,

where the true structural dose-response component is 

θATE (a) = a2 + 1 .2a. 

Image-based dSprites benchmark. We utilize the dSprites dataset [ 39 ], consisting of 64 × 64 images generated from latent factors: scale , rotation , posX , and posY . We follow the proximal causal adaptation proposed by Xu et al. [17]. 

• Treatment: The treatment A ∈ R4096 is a flattened dSprite image with additive Gaussian noise. 

• Causal Function: The target function is defined by a weighted quadratic of the image pixels: θATE (A) = (( vec (B)⊤A)2 − 3000) /500 , where Bij = |32 − j|/32 .

• Outcome: The response is Y = 12( posY − 0.5) 2θATE (A) + N (0 , 0.52).

• Proxies: The treatment proxy Z ∈ R3 consists of the ground-truth latent values for scale , rotation , and 

posX . The outcome proxy W is a separate image that shares the same posY as the treatment image, while its other latent factors are held at fixed reference values ( scale = 0.8, rotation = 0, posX = 0.5). 

Synthetic conditional dose-response benchmark. We use the same observational data-generating process as in the synthetic low-dimensional dose-response benchmark, but change the estimand to the conditional dose-response curve 

fATT (a; a′) := E[Y (a) | A = a′]

for a fixed anchor treatment level a′ ∈ R.

Observational data-generating process: Let 

U1 ∼ Unif[ −1, 2] , R ∼ Unif[0 , 1] ,

and define 

U2 := R − 1{0 ≤ U1 ≤ 1}.

The observed treatment, outcome, and proxy variables are generated as 

W1 = U2 + εW, 1, W2 = U1 + εW, 2,Z1 = U2 + εZ, 1, Z2 = U1 + εZ, 2,A = U1 + εA,

77 Y = 3 cos  2(0 .3U2 + 0 .3U1 + 0 .2) + 1 .5A + εY ,

where 

εW, 1, ε Z, 2 ∼ Unif[ −1, 1] , εW, 2, ε Z, 1, ε A, ε Y ∼ N (0 , 1) ,

and all exogenous variables are mutually independent. The treatment proxy is Z = ( Z1, Z 2), the outcome proxy is W = ( W1, W 2), and the latent confounding is driven by (U1, U 2).

Potential outcomes and the oracle ATT curve: Under the intervention A = a, the potential outcome is 

Y (a) = 3 cos  0.6U2 + 0 .6U1 + 0 .4 + 1 .5a + εY .

Since E[εY ] = 0 , the target ATT curve is 

fATT (a; a′) = E3 cos  0.6U2 + 0 .6U1 + 0 .4 + 1 .5a A = a′ .

• Third-stage regressions: MLP with hidden widths (32 , 64) , dropout 0.01 , MSE loss, 100 epochs, and weight decay 10 −6. The learning rate was 10 −3 for N = 2000 and 5 × 10 −4 for N ≥ 5000 .All proximal regularization parameters were exponentially annealed in all experiments.

Hyperparameters. All gradient-based featurizer updates and third-stage MLP updates used AdamW [ 58 ]; second-stage linear heads were refined using L-BFGS as described in Appendix E.1. We used the following configuration throughout this benchmark. 82 • Outcome bridge: first-stage loss = MSE; second-stage loss = log-cosh; learning rates 10 −4 for all featurizers; 100 epochs; 10 first-stage updates and 1 second-stage update per outer iteration; 5 L-BFGS steps for the second-stage head with learning rate 10 −2; weight decay 10 −5. The proximal regularization schedules were (5 × 10 −3, 10 −3) for the first-stage head, (10 −3, 5 × 10 −3) for the auxiliary first-stage solve inside the second stage, and (10 −2, 1) for the second-stage head. 

• Treatment bridge: first-stage loss = MSE; second-stage loss = log-cosh; learning rates 10 −3 for the first-stage featurizer and both second-stage featurizers; 100 epochs; 10 first-stage updates and 1 second-stage update per outer iteration; 5 L-BFGS steps for the second-stage head with learning rate 10 −2;weight decay 10 −6. The proximal regularization schedules were (5 × 10 −3, 10 −1) for the first-stage head, 

(5 × 10 −5, 10 −3) for the auxiliary first-stage solve, and (10 −3, 1) for the second-stage head. 

• Third-stage scalar regressions: input dimension 2, hidden widths (64 , 128) , dropout 0.05 , MSE loss, 

100 epochs, learning rate 10 −3, and weight decay 10 −6.All proximal regularization parameters were exponentially annealed in this benchmark. 

I.4 Further experiments, ablation studies, and compute resources 

Conditional dose-response on the low-dimensional benchmark We evaluate conditional dose-response estimation on the low-dimensional synthetic benchmark by changing the target from the population dose-response to fATT (a, a ′) = E[Y (a) | A = a′] for fixed anchor values a′ ∈ {− 1, −0.5, 0.5, 1}. The observational data-generating process is the same as in the low-dimensional dose-response experiment. The oracle curve is computed by numerical integration over the posterior law of the latent confounder conditional on A = a′ as described in Appendix I.2. Figure 3 reports the estimated conditional response curves for the four anchors. Across anchors, the doubly robust estimators almost always outperform the single-bridge neural estimators, OutcomeNet and TreatmentNet, confirming the benefit of combining outcome- and treatment-side bridge information. 

Controlled bridge misspecification analysis. We study controlled bridge misspecification on the synthetic low-dimensional dose-response benchmark. The goal is to assess whether the doubly robust estimator remains stable when one nuisance bridge is corrupted while the other is left unchanged. We fix the sample size to 

N = 5000 , first train both bridges normally, and then perturb only the final second-stage linear head of one bridge. For outcome misspecification, we set h ← h + |εh|, where the entries of εh are sampled independently from N (0 , σ 2); for treatment misspecification, we analogously set φ ← φ + |εφ|. The absolute value is taken entrywise. After perturbing one bridge, we train the doubly robust final-stage network using the perturbed bridge together with the unperturbed complementary bridge. We use noise levels σ ∈ { 0.2, 0.5}. Figure 4 reports the resulting mean estimated dose-response curves over repeated runs, with shaded bands indicating one standard deviation. This experiment isolates bridge-level nuisance misspecification and directly compares the single-bridge estimators with their doubly robust counterparts. Despite corrupting one bridge, the doubly robust estimators remain close to the ground-truth causal response curve, illustrating their robustness to single-bridge misspecification in this benchmark. 

Bridge robustness under asymmetric proxy informativeness. We conduct noisy-proxy experiments similar to Bozkurt et al. [21 , Section 13.5.1 ], where the informativeness of the treatment proxy Z and outcome proxy W is varied across six settings. Let Λ( t) = 0.81+exp( −t) + 0 .1. For Settings 1–2, U ∼ Beta (5 , 4) ; for Settings 3–4, U ∼ Beta (8 , 4) ; and for Settings 5–6, U ∼ Beta (3 , 5) . The kernel baselines reflect the designed proxy asymmetry for the single-bridge methods: KAP performs best in the odd-numbered settings, while KPV performs best in the even-numbered settings. However, in this finite-sample benchmark, DRKPV does not dominate the stronger single-bridge kernel baseline and is never the best kernel method across the six settings. The neural estimators are more stable across regimes: DRPCLNET-V1 improves on DRKPV in every setting, and one of the two DRPCLNET variants is the best neural method in four of the six settings. The doubly robust neural estimators do not uniformly dominate the best single-bridge neural ablation, since TreatmentNet is strongest in Settings 2 and 6, but they provide a competitive and stable combination of the outcome- and treatment-bridge routes under asymmetric proxy informativeness. 85 0.4 0.2 0.0 0.2 0.4

Figure 5: Heterogeneous response estimation under broken proxy–confounder links with N = 2000 . The transformations use W ← | W | + ϵW and/or Z ← | Z| + ϵZ , with entrywise Gaussian perturbations of standard deviation 100 .

Heterogeneous response under broken proxy links. We further stress-test heterogeneous response estimation by deliberately breaking the proxy–confounder links in the synthetic CATE benchmark. We fix the sample size to N = 2000 and consider three variants: a broken W –U link, a broken Z–U link, and a setting where both links are broken. Starting from the original proxies, we replace 

W ← | W | + ϵW , Z ← | Z| + ϵZ ,

where the absolute value is taken entrywise and the entries of ϵW and ϵZ are sampled independently from 

N (0 , 100 2). In the first variant only the transformation of W is applied, in the second only the transformation of Z is applied, and in the third both transformations are applied. Figure 5 shows the resulting heterogeneous response curves. The outcome-bridge estimator is relatively stable in these examples, whereas TreatmentNet degrades visibly when the W –U link is broken. The doubly robust estimators remain close to the oracle curve in the single-link break settings, while the setting where both links are broken should be interpreted only as a qualitative stress test rather than a setting covered by the bridge-identification assumptions. 

Second-stage loss ablation. We ablate the regression loss used in the second stage of the neural bridge estimators. We compare log-cosh, Huber, MSE optimized with L-BFGS refinement, and MSE with closed-form linear-head updates, denoted by MSE-CF. Unless explicitly labeled MSE-CF, the second-stage linear heads are refined by L-BFGS. The third-stage regressions use MSE in all cases, so this ablation isolates the effect of the second-stage bridge loss. Tables 11, 12, and 13 report the results at N = 20000 .Table 11: Second-stage loss ablation on the synthetic low-dimensional dose-response benchmark at N = 20000 .Entries are 10 2× causal MSE, reported as mean ± standard error. The loss choice has little effect on the low-dimensional and heterogeneous benchmarks: the doubly robust estimators remain stable across log-cosh, Huber, MSE, and MSE-CF, and consistently outperform the corre-sponding single-bridge neural estimators. The dSprites benchmark shows a clearer difference. Robust losses, especially log-cosh and Huber, improve over MSE and substantially reduce variability; MSE-CF narrows this gap but remains slightly worse than log-cosh for the doubly robust estimators. These results motivate our use of log-cosh as the default second-stage bridge loss in the main experiments. 

Compute resources. All experiments were run on a Linux x86_64 compute node with Python 3.12.12. GPU experiments used single-node SLURM jobs with one task, eight CPU cores, 10GB system memory, and a 24-hour wall-time limit. Unless otherwise stated, each job used a single NVIDIA RTX A4500 GPU with driver version 580.95.05 and CUDA 13.0; the GPU provided approximately 20GB of memory. 87
